Phase I Variables (polity-based)[edit]

General variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Alice Williams; Gréine Jordan ♥ Majority of work carried out by Alice Williams; Gréine Jordan wrote general description and edited some of Williams's work.

♠ Expert ♣ ♥


♠ UTM zone ♣ 14-Q ♥

♠ Original name ♣ Rosario ♥

♠ Alternative names ♣ Middle Preclassic; Middle Formative ♥

♠ Peak Date ♣ ♥


Temporal bounds[edit]

♠ Duration ♣ 700-500 BCE ♥ This phase is defined by the appearance of a new ceramic style (Rosario ceramics) in 700 BCE, and ends with the founding of the new settlement Monte Albán in the central region of the valley. The chiefdoms during this period are also more complex than in the preceding San José and Guadalupe phases.[1][2]

♠ Degree of centralization ♣ quasi-polity ♥ The largest of the three chiefdoms in the valley at this time was in the Etla arm and consisted of 18-20 villages focused around the central settlement (San José Mogote).[3] It is assumed that this network of settlements formed a complex chiefdom because of their proximity and hierarchy in relation to San José Mogote (the largest settlement). It is unlikely that there were alliances between the three chiefdoms based on evidence for violence and inter-village raiding between the three arms of the valley.[4]

♠ Supra-polity relations ♣ none ♥ The three chiefdoms were separate entities with no evidence for supra-polity relations.[5]

Supra-cultural relations[edit]

♠ preceding (quasi)polity ♣ MxSanGu ♥
♠ relationship to preceding (quasi)polity ♣ continuity ♥ The highest density of population continued to be in the Etla arm, with San José Mogote as the central settlement. The other arms of the valley, which were previously occupied, increased in population density and formed chiefdoms.[6]
♠ succeeding (quasi)polity ♣ MxAlb1E ♥
♠ Supracultural entity ♣ Middle Formative ♥
♠ scale of supra-cultural interaction ♣ [100-200] ♥ km squared. Similar pottery styles and symbols of prestige were shared between the Valley of Oaxaca and neighbouring Tehuacan Valley during this period, suggesting some interaction or cultural "system" which extended the distance between the valleys.[7]

♠ Capital ♣ San José Mogote ♥ San José Mogote was the largest site in the valley at this time, and may have been the central site of the chiefdom in the Etla arm.[8] Other smaller chiefly centers were Yegüih (in the eastern arm) and San Martin Tilcajete (in the southern arm).[9][10]

♠ Language ♣ ♥ There are no written records from this phase, but later evidence shows that people in the valley spoke Zapotec, possibly from the succeeding San José Mogote phase, and the Otomanguean language families may have split before the start of this period.[11]
♠ Linguistic Family ♣ Proto-Otomanguean ♥ Both the Zapotecan (Zapotec and Chatino) and Mixtecan (Mixtec, Cuicatec and Trique) languages belong to the Otomanguean language family, which also includes: Otopamean, Chinantecan Popolocan, Huave, Amuzgo and Manguean language groups.[12]. These language groups diverged between around 2100 BCE-1400 CE but it is not known exactly when each language or dialect split . It is however likely that Zapotecan and Mixtecan had diverged by 3700 BCE.[13]
♠ Language Genus ♣ ♥
♠ Religious Tradition ♣ ♥
♠ Religion Family ♣ ♥
♠ Religion Sect ♣ ♥


Map[edit]

General Description[edit]

During the Rosario phase (700-500 BCE), the settlement of San José Mogote continued to be the largest settlement in the Valley of Oaxaca. However, other settlements rose to challenge its primacy: Huitzo (at the extreme northern end of the Etla arm), Tilcajete (in the Valle Grande), and Yegüih (in the Tlacolula arm), each the centre of a cluster of smaller settlements.[14] There is evidence for increased inter-settlement conflict and social differentiation within communities.[15] Burnt remains of buildings have been found at Rosario phase sites, which, along with evidence for fortifications and the extensive unoccupied 'buffer zone' of 80 square kilometres between the polities, suggest inter-settlement raids and hostility at this time.[16]

Population and political organization

While there is evidence of an emerging elite during this period, the nature of leadership and political organization remains unclear.[17] Labour was organized for the construction of large public structures and elaborate tombs. However, the types of buildings constructed led archaeologists Kent Flannery and Joyce Marcus to suggest that elites could not yet draw on labour and resources solely for their own personal gain.[18][19] The first evidence of monumental art, calendars, writing and human sacrifice in the Valley of Oaxaca dates to this period.[20] Population estimates for the three polities in the valley at this time range from 1000 to 2000 people; the largest population was concentrated in the northern arm, with San José Mogote as the primary centre.[21][22]

Social Scale[edit]

♠ Polity territory ♣ [50-500] ♥ squared kilometers. "Where survey data are available, the political territory of these centers is known to range from 50 to 500 km2.”[23]

♠ Polity Population ♣ [1000-2000] ♥ People. Population estimates for the three chiefdoms in the valley at this time range from 1000-2000 people, with the largest population concentrated in the northern arm with San José Mogote as the primary center.[24][25] "The population of the Valley of Oaxaca did not grow appreciably throughout the Middle Formative period (ca. 850-500 BC)."[26] "Table 11.3. Population in the largest centers, by phase, in Oaxaca and Ejutla."[27] Valley of Oaxaca population (Largest center in Oaxaca): Tierras Largas: 327 (128); San Jose: 1942 (1384); Guadalupe: 1788 (774); Rosario: 1835 (564); Early I: 14652 (5250); Late I: 51339 (17242); Monte Alban II: 41927 (14492); Monte Alban IIIA: 120121 (16507); Monte Alban IIIB: 78930 (24189); Monte Alban IV: 77612 (16117); Monte Alban V: 166467 (13831).[28]

♠ Population of the largest settlement ♣ 1000 ♥ Inhabitants. Estimated population at San José Mogote is around 1000 people (60-65ha).[29][30] "The population of the Valley of Oaxaca did not grow appreciably throughout the Middle Formative period (ca. 850-500 BC). San Jose Mogote decreased in size but still remained the region's largest settlement with the most elaborate monumental construction."[31] "Table 11.3. Population in the largest centers, by phase, in Oaxaca and Ejutla."[32] Valley of Oaxaca population (Largest center in Oaxaca): Rosario: 1835 (564).[33]

Hierarchical Complexity[edit]

♠ Settlement hierarchy ♣ 3 ♥ levels. The settlement hierarchy is based on the size of settlements and the presence or absence of public buildings.[34]

1. Primary center

In addition to San Jose Mogote, in this period "several smaller centers with public architecture were established in the other arms of the valley", e.g. Yeguih, Tilcajete, Tlapacoyan.[35] P
Yeguih and Tilcajete have also been called primary centers (San José Mogote, Yegüih, San Martin Tilcajete)35-60ha[36].
San Jose Mogote had large public buildings, including Structures 28 (a temple) and 19 (a platform for the temple and other buildings).[37]
2. Smaller centers
"This basic settlement patterna dominant center surrounded by a cluster of smaller settlements and lower densities of population at greater distances from the center"[38]
Secondary centres (e.g. Huitzo, Tomaltepec, El Mogote)El Mogote was 25ha[39] and these sites had their own public buildings (such as the 3m high adobe structure at Tomaltepec)[40]
3. Satellite settlements of the smaller centers
"Clusters of settlements were established around these new centers, which may have been the head towns of small, spatially discrete polities that had variable degrees of autonomy from San Jose Mogote (Blanton et al., 1999, pp. 43-44)."[41]
Villages (e.g. Fábrica San José, San Sebastián Abasolo, Tierras Largas).95-3ha[42]

♠ Administrative levels ♣ [1-2] ♥ levels. Possibly two could be inferred, based on the number of levels in the settlement hierarchy and the logistics required to import large limestone blocks for public buildings at San José Mogote (particularly Structure 19 which was possibly the largest and measured roughly 22x28m in extent). The stone came from a quarry at Rancho Matadamas which was 5km from San José Mogote.[43][44] This division may, however, be incorrect as it should not be assumed that the settlement hierarchy corresponds with the administrative hierarchy for this period (the sites may have been competing with each other rather than in dominant or subordinate administrative positions).[45]

1. Main administrative centre, corresponding with primary centres of the settlement hierarchy (e.g. San José Mogote)

2. Secondary administrative centres, corresponding with other smaller settlements with evidence for public architecture

♠ Religious levels ♣ 1 ♥ level. sources do not suggest there is evidence for standardised religion during this period (standardised temples with private sacred rooms appear in later periods). A primary centre, San José Mogote, had two structures (19 and 28) which may have supported a temple, as well as a circular platform (structure 31). Smaller platform constructions were present at secondary centres, although the religious connection with the larger centres is not clear.[46]

♠ Military levels ♣ 1 ♥ levels. At least one level of military organisation is inferred, based on evidence for inter-village raiding.[47] The raids would have been small scale, and military leadership is unlikely to have been a permanent position.

Professions[edit]

♠ Occupational complexity ♣ ♥

♠ Professional military officers ♣ absent ♥ Warfare consisted of small-scale raiding during this period, and sources do not suggest there is evidence for professional military officers or soldiers.[48][49]

♠ Professional soldiers ♣ absent ♥ Warfare consisted of small-scale raiding during this period, and sources do not suggest there is evidence for professional military officers or soldiers.[50][51]

♠ Professional priesthood ♣ suspected unknown ♥ There is evidence for temples at primary and secondary settlements in the Etla arm of the valley.[52] Unclear whether priests were professional.

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ absent ♥ Sources do not suggest there is evidence for full-time bureaucrats during this period.[53]

♠ Source of support ♣ absent ♥ Sources do not suggest there is evidence for full-time bureaucrats during this period.[54]

♠ Examination system ♣ absent ♥ Sources do not suggest there is evidence for full-time bureaucrats during this period.[55]

♠ Merit promotion ♣ absent ♥ Sources do not suggest there is evidence for full-time bureaucrats during this period.[56]

♠ Specialized government buildings ♣ absent ♥ The largest buildings constructed during this period were used as temples and elite residences rather than for specific governmental purposes.[57]

Law[edit]

♠ Formal legal code ♣ absent ♥ A formal legal code is assumed absent as the only evidence for writing during this period is from the carved stone slab (Monument 3) at San José Mogote.[58]

♠ Judges ♣ inferred absent ♥ A formal legal system, as associated buildings and lawyers, is assumed absent as the only evidence for writing during this period is from the carved stone slab (Monument 3) at San José Mogote[59] and the largest buildings were used as temples and elite residences.[60]

♠ Courts ♣ inferred absent ♥ A formal legal system, as associated buildings and lawyers, is assumed absent as the only evidence for writing during this period is from the carved stone slab (Monument 3) at San José Mogote[61] and the largest buildings were used as temples and elite residences.[62]

♠ Professional Lawyers ♣ inferred absent ♥ A formal legal system, as associated buildings and lawyers, is assumed absent as the only evidence for writing during this period is from the carved stone slab (Monument 3) at San José Mogote[63] and the largest buildings were used as temples and elite residences.[64]

Specialized Buildings: polity owned[edit]

♠ Communal buildings ♣ present ♥ Non-residential buildings were constructed at San José Mogote and other sites in the valley (such as Huitzo, a 3ha village[65]), but the use of some of these buildings is unknown. For example, Structure 31 on Mound 1 at San José Mogote is a round platform which may have been used for dancing or combat, based on the use of similar structures in later periods. Other buildings include structures 14 and 19, with Monument 3 (the image of a captive carved into a stone slab) placed in a corridor between the two buildings. The use of the former is unknown as the building has been disturbed by later constructions and the reuse of blocks. The later formed the base for a large wattle-and-daub temple.[66]

♠ Special purpose house ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The buildings during this period were primarily residential, and the specific function of non-residential buildings cannot be reliably identified until the architectural styles of the Monte Alban II phase appear.[67]

♠ Utilitarian public buildings ♣ absent ♥ Evidence for terracing systems and irrigation appear in later periods after the founding of Monte Alban and the Zapotec polity.

♠ irrigation systems ♣ absent ♥ There is very little evidence for irrigation systems during this period. Most settlements were located on the high alluvium and so would not have needed irrigation to supply the crops (only in later periods, when settlements were established on the piedmont, did irrigation become more prevalent).[68][69][70]
♠ drinking water supply systems ♣ ♥
♠ markets ♣ absent ♥ There is very little evidence to suggest the presence of any market system before the establishment of Monte Alban.[71]
♠ food storage sites ♣ absent ♥ Sources do not suggest there is evidence for polity-owned storage sites during this period.[72]

♠ Symbolic buildings ♣ ♥ Numerous non-residential buildings were constructed on Mound 1 at San José Mogote during this period, including temple platforms (e.g. Structure 19) next to Monument 3 (the stone carving of a captive).[73]

♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ present ♥ Structure 31 was a circular platform, 50-60 cm high, which may have been used for dancing or for combat.[74]

♠ Knowledge/information buildings ♣ absent ♥ The only evidence for writing during this period is from the glyphs carved on Monument 3; sources do not suggest there is evidence for the recording of information.[75]

Transport infrastructure[edit]

♠ Roads ♣ absent ♥ Sources do not suggest there is evidence for the construction of roads during this period.[76] Gary Feinman[77] told us that "Between sites there are known 16th century trails, which were likely used for a long, long time. [..] they likely were not paved, but there were no beasts of burden." However, we do not count paths and trails not constructed deliberately as roads.
♠ Bridges ♣ inferred absent ♥ Sources do not suggest there is evidence for the construction of bridges during this period.[78]
♠ Canals ♣ inferred absent ♥ Sources do not suggest there is evidence for the construction of canals during this period (although some small scale irrigation practices may have been used).[79]
♠ Ports ♣ absent ♥ The Valley of Oaxaca is landlocked.

Platform roughly 22 by 29 meters. Staircase lead to another platform (Structure 28) on which stood a 'wattle and daub' temple.[82]

Writing System[edit]

♠ Mnemonic devices ♣ inferred absent ♥ Sources do not suggest that evidence for writing other than carved glyphs has been found.[100]
♠ Nonwritten records ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Written records ♣ present ♥ Glyphs on a stone slab (Monument 3) may refer to the name of the captive depicted there and calendric dates.[101][102] Mesoamerican civilizations, including the Maya, Aztec, Mixtec and Zapotec all possessed "a true form of writing: a series of hieroglyphs arranged in vertical columns and in many instances combined with numerals. The glyphs were at least indirectly related to a spoken language." Zapotec and Mixtec belong to the Otomanguean language family while the Aztec and and Maya belong to the Utoaztecan and Macro-Mayan, respectively. Zapotec writing system is considered the oldest (from c600 BCE). Zapotec inscriptions are considered true writing, since the inscriptions had verbs.[103]
♠ Script ♣ present ♥ Glyphs.[104]
♠ Non-phonetic writing ♣ present ♥ Glyphs.[105]
♠ Phonetic alphabetic writing ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs.[106]

Kinds of Written Documents[edit]

♠ Lists, tables, and classifications ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[107][108]
♠ Calendar ♣ present ♥ Glyphs carved onto Monument 3 at San Jose Mogote refer to the 260-day ritual calendar, based on analogy with later periods.[109][110]
♠ Sacred Texts ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[111][112]
♠ Religious literature ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[113][114]
♠ Practical literature ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[115][116]
♠ History ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[117][118]
♠ Philosophy ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[119][120]
♠ Scientific literature ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[121][122]
♠ Fiction ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[123][124]

Military Technologies[edit]

Military use of Metals[edit]

♠ Copper ♣ absent ♥ Metalworking was not widely used in Mesoamerica, with metal products consisting mainly of small beads and ornaments.[137][138]
♠ Bronze ♣ absent ♥ Metalworking was not widely used in Mesoamerica, with metal products consisting mainly of small beads and ornaments.[139][140]
♠ Iron ♣ absent ♥ Metalworking was not widely used in Mesoamerica, with metal products consisting mainly of small beads and ornaments.[141][142]
♠ Steel ♣ absent ♥ Metalworking was not widely used in Mesoamerica, with metal products consisting mainly of small beads and ornaments.[143][144]

Projectiles[edit]

♠ Javelins ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources[145] only mention very little archaeological evidence for weaponry for this period, and this does not include javelins. However, weapons made from wood and cloth have been documented for the later periods, so their absence in the archaeological record may be due to preservation bias.
♠ Other thrown weapons ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources[146] only mention very little archaeological evidence for weaponry for this period, and this does not include thrown weapons. However, weapons made from wood and cloth have been documented for the later periods, so their absence in the archaeological record may be due to preservation bias.
♠ Atlatl ♣ present ♥ Present in the valley of Oaxaca since preceramic times (the Proto-Otomangueans) for hunting.[147] and obsidian blades were found in Tomb 10 at San José Mogote which may have been hafted into atlatl darts.[148] In addition, glyphs depicting what may be atlatls or spearthrowers have been carved with the danzantes at San Jose Mogote.[149]
♠ Slings ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources[150] only mention very little archaeological evidence for weaponry for this period, and this does not include slings. However, weapons made from wood and cloth have been documented for the later periods, so their absence in the archaeological record may be due to preservation bias. Moreover, Spanish documents record the use of slings at the end of the Monte Alban V period.[151]
♠ Self bow ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources[152] only mention very little archaeological evidence for weaponry for this period, and this does not include bows of any kind. However, weapons made from wood and cloth have been documented for the later periods, so their absence in the archaeological record may be due to preservation bias. Moreover, Spanish documents record the use of bows and arrows at the end of the Monte Alban V period.[153]
♠ Composite bow ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources[154] only mention very little archaeological evidence for weaponry for this period, and this does not include bows of any kind. However, weapons made from wood and cloth have been documented for the later periods, so their absence in the archaeological record may be due to preservation bias. Moreover, Spanish documents record the use of bows and arrows at the end of the Monte Alban V period.[155]
♠ Crossbow ♣ absent ♥ Hassig lists crossbows among the new military technologies the Spanish introduced to the region in the sixteenth century[156]
♠ Tension siege engines ♣ absent ♥
♠ Sling siege engines ♣ absent ♥
♠ Incendiaries ♣ absent ♥
♠ Gunpowder siege artillery ♣ absent ♥
♠ Handheld firearms ♣ absent ♥

♠ War clubs ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources[157] only mention very little archaeological evidence for weaponry for this period, and this does not include clubs. However, weapons made from wood and cloth have been documented for the later periods, so their absence in the archaeological record may be due to preservation bias.

However, weapons made from wood and cloth have been documented for the later periods, so their absence in the archaeological record may be due to preservation bias.
♠ Daggers ♣ present ♥ Obsidian-edged wooden swords and daggers are inferred present based the presence of obsidian blades in the valley.[159]
♠ Swords ♣ present ♥ Obsidian-edged wooden swords and daggers are inferred present based the presence of obsidian blades in the valley.[160]
♠ Spears ♣ inferred present ♥ Present in the valley of Oaxaca since preceramic times (the Proto-Otomangueans) for hunting.[161] However, it does seem to be clear whether they were also used as weapons in warfare.
♠ Polearms ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources[162] only mention very little archaeological evidence for weaponry for this period, and this does not include polearms. However, weapons made from wood and cloth have been documented for the later periods, so their absence in the archaeological record may be due to preservation bias.

Ritual variables[edit]

A collective ritual is a ritual in which two or more people participate. With the official cult we refer to the set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any).

♠ RA ♣ Rosalind Purcell; Enrico Cioni ♥ RP first coded this section; EC supplemented and/or replaced RP's work.

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Description of the Ritual System

The Rosario polity was a continuation of the San Jose polity. San Jose Magote remained the largest urban area and the ritual system continued to develop the Earth and Sky deities. The Earth was a benevolent spirit, but it disliked having crops removed, the digging of post holes or being burnt. It would respond in anger through earthquakes. The Sky was full of celestial spirits and ancestors in the form of clouds, if angry it would respond with lightning. "Earth and Sky appear to have been the ancestors of certain male descent groups in San Jose phase villages." [232]

Polity is coded below for archaeological information, due to poor understanding of individual rituals.

Rites of passage Birth Cranial deformationhigh status infants with cranial deformation found, children were either strapped to a fixed occipital board with a board on the forehead (tabular) or had a band tied tight around their head (anular). Tabluar deformation was the most common in the graves and could occur to male and female. One girl had annular deformation, possibly from another tribe, she was also buried atypically. [233]

Death BurialspresentAt Tomaltepec more than 60 graves found, some have two skeletonsthe first evidence of husband and wife burials and people being treated as a pair rather than individuals. Evidence to suggest that people were dug up and reburied with their spouses. No infants or childrenonly those old enough to have gone through initiation rituals. Six men were buried in a kneeling position (all others extended prone) which was so tight they must have been tied or wrapped. They contained half the vessels in the cemetery and most of the jade beads and it is likely they were the community leaders. [234] In small cemetery in San Jose Magote remains of all ages found. Most were buried fully extended and face down. Nearly all adults had a single jade bead in the mouth. Husband and wife pairs were present. [235]

Grave goodspresentvessels containing highly stylised images of Earthquake and Lightning, probably the supernatural forces believed in. They are found in male burials only and are probably representative of ancestor groups Earth and Sky. [236]


Gatherings Ritual Space ArchitecturepresentStructures I and 3 at San Jose Mogote are pyramidal structures, probably the bases for a wattle and daub public building. They are the first known use of adobe brick in Oaxaca. Structure 8 dates to the Guadelupe phase. Huitzo also contained several public buildings of rival size to San Jose Magote, despite a lesser population. [237] Size - Structure 1rose in stages to 2.5m high structure 21m high, 18m wide. Structure 8retaining walls of 1m x 0.75m tall Structure 42m high, 15m wide platform, with series of one room temples including Structure 3 1.3 x 11.5m topped by wattle and daub building. Symbolic Carvings Structure 2two carved stones from structure 2, one depicts a feline, the other a raptorial bird

Feasting Animal bone depositspresentfrom Tierras Largas, Feature 99, a number of domestic dog remains found. Different parts of the dog were grouped together, e.g. forelegs, skull, hind legs, suggesting specific matching of people to parts of the animal. [238] Butcherypresentall dog remains were systematically butchered Frequencyrarepresence of such extensive consummation of meat, at least 50kg,is rare in Guadelupe phase.

Anthropomorphic Representations PresentMiniature stools found, which were likely to be used with figurines, as found at nearby Chiapas. Stools kept figures/humans off the ground and raised them above other people, thereby indicating superiority.

Figurines also found which fall into dichotomy similar to burialsfigures are either lying extended or sitting upright, legs crossed. It is likely a continuation of the idea that those sitting above hold powers over those below. It is suggested that the figurines were used by females in rituals invoking ancestors.

Figures may have been arranged into small ritual scenes, such as was buried beneath the floor of House 16, San Jose Magote. Three figures lay extended, arms crossed on chests, a fourth figure on top is in the seated position, though on its side, probably because it fell over when buried. [239]



Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ public rituals ♥ "I argue that Mound 1 represents a further 'scaling up' of public architecture and ritual performance, which engaged larger numbers of people in practices which defined the San José Mogote community and its broader polity. The evidence suggests that at the core of this corporate identity was the relationship between the community and the divine with sacrifice and perhaps feasting and dance as rituals through which people contacted the sacred." [240] "I argue that Mound 1 represents a further 'scaling up' of public architecture and ritual performance, which engaged larger numbers of people in practices which defined the San José Mogote community and its broader polity. The evidence suggests that at the core of this corporate identity was the relationship between the community and the divine with sacrifice and perhaps feasting and dance as rituals through which people contacted the sacred. "[...] "Although people used autosacrificial bloodletting to make offerings to the deities as early as the Early Formative, the innovation of human sacrifice represents a new and more spectacular ritual practice used to communicate with the divine. It is likely that the performance of human sacrifice was restricted to Rosario-phase nobles, given that evidence for this practice was associated only with public buildings and elite residences and that only nobles carried out the ritual in later periods (Boone 1984; Schele & Miller 1986). Nobles acted as intermediaries between people and the sacred realm with human sacrifice as a new ritual form probably linked to warfare. Elaborate obsidian bloodletters and the anthropomorphic brazier suggest the presence of luxury goods and ritual paraphernalia restricted to nobles." [241] "Despite the emergence of hereditary nobles, it is likely that the construction of monumental buildings, the enactment of public ceremonies like human sacrifice, and the pursuit of warfare were probably still seen in communal terms. Given that hereditary inequality emerged out of a more communal and less hierarchical discourse, it is reasonable to assume that the power of nobles was dependent on their being seen as acting on the behalf of their communities. These early nobles undoubtedly had ritual and political obligations to their communities like Zapotec and Mixtec rulers at the time of the Spanish Conquest. It is likely that nobles earned the allegiance of commoners through the sponsoring of public ceremonies, including ritual feasting, as well as through success in warfare and perhaps the gifting of certain luxury goods. Nobles deployed human sacrifice as another way to maintain allegiance since it was the most potent way in which the sacred covenant could be activated to petition deities for fertility and prosperity on behalf of the community. Given the physical setting of Monument 3, the audience for these sacrifices was probably restricted, much like the setting of Middle Formative iconography dealing with human sacrifice in other parts of Mesoamerica (A. Joyce 2008b). By taking captives and offering them as sacrifices to the gods, nobles at San José Mogote demonstrated their ritual and political power as well as generosity to their communities. Human sacrifice was also a dramatic ritual performance in both its sacred and its violent qualities that would have bound people to the deities, ancestors, and other symbols of the community on whose behalf the sacrifice was realized." [242]

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ women's rituals ♥ NOTE: Women's rituals are here considered "most widespread" because, unlike other rituals, which were confined to specialised buildings, these ones were performed in virtually every house. Moreover, though Marcus reconstructs two possible women's rituals, only one can be coded. The divination ritual has been chosen because it is very slightly better understood. However, relevant passages describing the figurine ritual have been included here because it might be useful. "Although there is no sign of women’s tools or activities near our early public buildings, we do find evidence for women’s ritual in three places: the house (yoho quixi or yoho coba); the associated ramada or lean-to (yoho yaha or yoho yaa); and the surrounding dooryard. These activities are believed to include (1) divination by the casting of beans or maize kernels, and (2) communication with recent ancestors through the use of small solid figurines. "Let us begin with divination, which—by analogy with ethnohistoric descriptions—may have been carried out in special basins in the dooryard of the house. Such basins were waterproofed with a layer of lime plaster, then painted in one of the colors associated with the four Zapotec world directions: red, yellow, white, or black. Two such painted basins were discovered in the yard of a San José phase house, Household C3, at San José Mogote (Fig. 9). East of the house was a red-painted basin, while a yellow-painted basin lay 3 m to the south. The red-painted basin was 1.2 m in diameter and recessed 5 cm into the ground; it had been mud-plastered, given a waterproof coating of lime, then painted red with specular hematite. The yellow-painted basin was similar in every way except color. Whether there were once four basins—each painted a different color and oriented to one of the four world directions—is not known, since exposing the relevant areas of the yard would have required the destruction of a later building (Marcus 1989: fig. 8.20). "We know from sixteenth-century documents that Zapotec diviners sat on a mat and cast maize kernels onto the surface of a water-filled basin, noting whether they fell or floated in groups of 2, 3, 5, or 13. This use of water (niça) for casting lots was called tiniyaaya niça, and it was used to determine the cause and cure of illnesses affecting household members; to predict whether a particular day was auspicious for planting or marriage; or to assign a name to a newborn child. We suspect that the painted basins of Household C3 may have served for such divination (Fig. 10). "Zapotec women also petitioned their recently deceased ancestors for guidance, often doing so in or near the house where that ancestor had lived. We suspect, on the basis of ethnographic analogy, that small solid figurines— of which we have found hundreds in household context—provided the medium to which the spirits of specific ancestors could return during this petitioning. Women preferred to petition their female ancestors, which may account for the fact that the majority of these figurines are women." [261] "Women’s ritual seems to have been conducted in the house, the adjacent lean-to, and the surrounding dooryard. The recently deceased ancestors—both males and females, but especially females—appear to have been the focus of much of this ritual. Small solid figurines, sometimes arranged in ritual scenes, evidently provided a venue to which the spirits of the ancestors could return; in that venue they were probably consulted, petitioned, and involved in the ongoing affairs of their descendants. Women also made figurines of animals (principally dogs and birds), which may have served the ancestors as “animal agents” like those described for China." [262] "In later periods in China, animals continued to be important as lineage emblems, playing roles in ritual and in communicating with the Sky, Earth, and the dead (Chang 1980: 209).The Shang-period Chinese (1200-1000 b.c.) depicted animals on bronze vessels, including dragons or snakes. Such depictions were called wu, a term that has been translated “animal offerings.”According to K. C. Chang (1983: 65): 'to make animal offerings was a concrete means of achieving communication between heaven and earth, the dead and the living. We thus come to the inevitable conclusion that the animal designs on Shang and Chou bronzes are iconographically meaningful: they are images of the various animals that served as the helpers of shamans and shamanesses in the task of communication between heaven and earth, the spirits and the living.'" [263]

♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ monthly ♥ "Most likely, this ritual was performed simply "to determine the cause and cure of illnesses affecting household members; to predict whether a particular day was auspicious for planting or marriage; or to assign a name to a newborn child." [266]

Most frequent collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ women's rituals ♥ NOTE: Though Marcus reconstructs two possible women's rituals, only one can be coded. The divination ritual has been chosen because it is very slightly better understood. However, relevant passages describing the figurine ritual have been included here because it might be useful. "Although there is no sign of women’s tools or activities near our early public buildings, we do find evidence for women’s ritual in three places: the house (yoho quixi or yoho coba); the associated ramada or lean-to (yoho yaha or yoho yaa); and the surrounding dooryard. These activities are believed to include (1) divination by the casting of beans or maize kernels, and (2) communication with recent ancestors through the use of small solid figurines. "Let us begin with divination, which—by analogy with ethnohistoric descriptions—may have been carried out in special basins in the dooryard of the house. Such basins were waterproofed with a layer of lime plaster, then painted in one of the colors associated with the four Zapotec world directions: red, yellow, white, or black. Two such painted basins were discovered in the yard of a San José phase house, Household C3, at San José Mogote (Fig. 9). East of the house was a red-painted basin, while a yellow-painted basin lay 3 m to the south. The red-painted basin was 1.2 m in diameter and recessed 5 cm into the ground; it had been mud-plastered, given a waterproof coating of lime, then painted red with specular hematite. The yellow-painted basin was similar in every way except color. Whether there were once four basins—each painted a different color and oriented to one of the four world directions—is not known, since exposing the relevant areas of the yard would have required the destruction of a later building (Marcus 1989: fig. 8.20). "We know from sixteenth-century documents that Zapotec diviners sat on a mat and cast maize kernels onto the surface of a water-filled basin, noting whether they fell or floated in groups of 2, 3, 5, or 13. This use of water (niça) for casting lots was called tiniyaaya niça, and it was used to determine the cause and cure of illnesses affecting household members; to predict whether a particular day was auspicious for planting or marriage; or to assign a name to a newborn child. We suspect that the painted basins of Household C3 may have served for such divination (Fig. 10). "Zapotec women also petitioned their recently deceased ancestors for guidance, often doing so in or near the house where that ancestor had lived. We suspect, on the basis of ethnographic analogy, that small solid figurines— of which we have found hundreds in household context—provided the medium to which the spirits of specific ancestors could return during this petitioning. Women preferred to petition their female ancestors, which may account for the fact that the majority of these figurines are women." [289] "Women’s ritual seems to have been conducted in the house, the adjacent lean-to, and the surrounding dooryard. The recently deceased ancestors—both males and females, but especially females—appear to have been the focus of much of this ritual. Small solid figurines, sometimes arranged in ritual scenes, evidently provided a venue to which the spirits of the ancestors could return; in that venue they were probably consulted, petitioned, and involved in the ongoing affairs of their descendants. Women also made figurines of animals (principally dogs and birds), which may have served the ancestors as “animal agents” like those described for China." [290] "In later periods in China, animals continued to be important as lineage emblems, playing roles in ritual and in communicating with the Sky, Earth, and the dead (Chang 1980: 209).The Shang-period Chinese (1200-1000 b.c.) depicted animals on bronze vessels, including dragons or snakes. Such depictions were called wu, a term that has been translated “animal offerings.”According to K. C. Chang (1983: 65): 'to make animal offerings was a concrete means of achieving communication between heaven and earth, the dead and the living. We thus come to the inevitable conclusion that the animal designs on Shang and Chou bronzes are iconographically meaningful: they are images of the various animals that served as the helpers of shamans and shamanesses in the task of communication between heaven and earth, the spirits and the living.'" [291]

♠ Frequency per participant ♣ ♥ daily/weekly/monthly/yearly/once per generation/once in a lifetime. Code the frequency of participating in a ritual from the perspective of the participants. This group of participants does not include the ritual specialists, like priests. Highly engaged audiences (audiences who are processing, jeering, ...) are considered as participants. Audiences consisting of passive spectators or witnesses are coded for in the next variable. When it is difficult to distinguish between a highly engaged audience and an audience consisting of passive spectators, code these audiences here.

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ cranial deformation ♥ Perhaps this should not count as dysphoric since it took place in childhood, but in the absence of any other evidence of dysphoric ritual it is coded here. Cranial deformation occurred in high status children. Children were either strapped to a fixed occipital board with a board on the forehead (tabular) or had a band tied tight around their head (anular). Tabluar deformation was the most common in the graves and could occur to male and female. One girl had annular deformation, possibly from another tribeshe was also buried atypically. [317]

Phase II Variables (polity-based)[edit]

Institutional Variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Gréine Jordan ♥

♠ Editor ♣ ♥

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

Legal (formal) limits[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ inferred absent ♥ While there is evidence of an emerging elite the nature of leadership is still unclear. Given the focus on raiding it is unlikely that rulers were separate from the ‘military’. ‘The brazier and tomb still do not constitute definitive evidence of a system of ranked descent groups, but they do suggest that by the Rosario phase there may have been more use of a rhetoric that claimed a connection between wealth, status, power, and ancestors. The use of the term "chiefdom" might be appropriate for this social formation, with the proviso that we still cannot be sure that ranked descent groups were part of the social matrix.’ [324] More influential members of society also began to emerge more prominently during this period, who organised labour for the construction of large public structures and elaborate tombs. However, the types of buildings constructed suggest that elites could not yet use labour and resources just for their own personal gain (as elaborate as the elite residences were, Flannery and Marcus[325] believe that they could have been constructed by the elite family without the need for corvée labour). This next step in social hierarchy appeared later when the Zapotec state had been firmly established.[326] At least one level of military organisation is inferred, based on evidence for inter-village raiding.[327] The raids would have been small scale, and military leadership is unlikely to have been a permanent position. From Ritual: '"Despite the emergence of hereditary nobles, it is likely that the construction of monumental buildings, the enactment of public ceremonies like human sacrifice, and the pursuit of warfare were probably still seen in communal terms. Given that hereditary inequality emerged out of a more communal and less hierarchical discourse, it is reasonable to assume that the power of nobles was dependent on their being seen as acting on the behalf of their communities. These early nobles undoubtedly had ritual and political obligations to their communities like Zapotec and Mixtec rulers at the time of the Spanish Conquest. It is likely that nobles earned the allegiance of commoners through the sponsoring of public ceremonies, including ritual feasting, as well as through success in warfare and perhaps the gifting of certain luxury goods. Nobles deployed human sacrifice as another way to maintain allegiance since it was the most potent way in which the sacred covenant could be activated to petition deities for fertility and prosperity on behalf of the community. Given the physical setting of Monument 3, the audience for these sacrifices was probably restricted, much like the setting of Middle Formative iconography dealing with human sacrifice in other parts of Mesoamerica (A. Joyce 2008b). By taking captives and offering them as sacrifices to the gods, nobles at San José Mogote demonstrated their ritual and political power as well as generosity to their communities. Human sacrifice was also a dramatic ritual performance in both its sacred and its violent qualities that would have bound people to the deities, ancestors, and other symbols of the community on whose behalf the sacrifice was realized." [328] The chiefdoms during this period are also more complex than in the preceding San José and Guadalupe phases.[329][330] The largest of the three chiefdoms in the valley at this time was in the Etla arm and consisted of 18-20 villages focused around the central settlement (San José Mogote).[331] It is assumed that this network of settlements formed a complex chiefdom because of their proximity and hierarchy in relation to San José Mogote (the largest settlement). It is unlikely that there were alliances between the three chiefdoms based on evidence for violence and inter-village raiding between the three arms of the valley.[332]Other smaller chiefly centers were Yegüih (in the eastern arm) and San Martin Tilcajete (in the southern arm).[333][334] [335]. Burnt remains of buildings have been found at Rosario phase sites, which, along with evidence for fortifications and the extensive 80km2 unoccupied “buffer zone” between the chiefdoms, suggest inter-settlement raids and hostility at this time.[336] San José Mogote, previously the largest settlement in the valley, became the centre of the Etla chiefdom with a network of 18-20 villages.[337]. San Martin Tilcajete (25ha) rivalled San José Mogote in the southern subvalley, as did Yegüih in the eastern subvalley.[338]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ suspected unknown ♥ While there is evidence of an emerging elite the nature of leadership is still unclear as is the nature/presence of a legislative. ‘The brazier and tomb still do not constitute definitive evidence of a system of ranked descent groups, but they do suggest that by the Rosario phase there may have been more use of a rhetoric that claimed a connection between wealth, status, power, and ancestors. Joyce 2008b). By taking captives and offering them as sacrifices to the gods, nobles at San José Mogote demonstrated their ritual and political power as well as generosity to their communities. Human sacrifice was also a dramatic ritual performance in both its sacred and its violent qualities that would have bound people to the deities, ancestors, and other symbols of the community on whose behalf the sacrifice was realized." [355] The chiefdoms during this period are also more complex than in the preceding San José and Guadalupe phases.[356][357] The largest of the three chiefdoms in the valley at this time was in the Etla arm and consisted of 18-20 villages focused around the central settlement (San José Mogote).[358] It is assumed that this network of settlements formed a complex chiefdom because of their proximity and hierarchy in relation to San José Mogote (the largest settlement). It is unlikely that there were alliances between the three chiefdoms based on evidence for violence and inter-village raiding between the three arms of the valley.[359]Other smaller chiefly centers were Yegüih (in the eastern arm) and San Martin Tilcajete (in the southern arm).[360][361] [362]

Legal (formal) freedoms[edit]

♠ Right to petition executive ♣ suspected unknown ♥ While there is evidence of an emerging elite the nature of leadership is still unclear. From Ritual: '"Despite the emergence of hereditary nobles, it is likely that the construction of monumental buildings, the enactment of public ceremonies like human sacrifice, and the pursuit of warfare were probably still seen in communal terms. Given that hereditary inequality emerged out of a more communal and less hierarchical discourse, it is reasonable to assume that the power of nobles was dependent on their being seen as acting on the behalf of their communities. These early nobles undoubtedly had ritual and political obligations to their communities like Zapotec and Mixtec rulers at the time of the Spanish Conquest. It is likely that nobles earned the allegiance of commoners through the sponsoring of public ceremonies, including ritual feasting, as well as through success in warfare and perhaps the gifting of certain luxury goods. Nobles deployed human sacrifice as another way to maintain allegiance since it was the most potent way in which the sacred covenant could be activated to petition deities for fertility and prosperity on behalf of the community. Given the physical setting of Monument 3, the audience for these sacrifices was probably restricted, much like the setting of Middle Formative iconography dealing with human sacrifice in other parts of Mesoamerica (A. Joyce 2008b). By taking captives and offering them as sacrifices to the gods, nobles at San José Mogote demonstrated their ritual and political power as well as generosity to their communities. Human sacrifice was also a dramatic ritual performance in both its sacred and its violent qualities that would have bound people to the deities, ancestors, and other symbols of the community on whose behalf the sacrifice was realized." [363]

Customary (informal) checks on executive[edit]

♠ Informal critique of executive ♣ suspected unknown ♥ While there is evidence of an emerging elite the nature of leadership is still unclear. From Ritual: '"Despite the emergence of hereditary nobles, it is likely that the construction of monumental buildings, the enactment of public ceremonies like human sacrifice, and the pursuit of warfare were probably still seen in communal terms. Given that hereditary inequality emerged out of a more communal and less hierarchical discourse, it is reasonable to assume that the power of nobles was dependent on their being seen as acting on the behalf of their communities. These early nobles undoubtedly had ritual and political obligations to their communities like Zapotec and Mixtec rulers at the time of the Spanish Conquest. It is likely that nobles earned the allegiance of commoners through the sponsoring of public ceremonies, including ritual feasting, as well as through success in warfare and perhaps the gifting of certain luxury goods. Nobles deployed human sacrifice as another way to maintain allegiance since it was the most potent way in which the sacred covenant could be activated to petition deities for fertility and prosperity on behalf of the community. Given the physical setting of Monument 3, the audience for these sacrifices was probably restricted, much like the setting of Middle Formative iconography dealing with human sacrifice in other parts of Mesoamerica (A. Joyce 2008b). By taking captives and offering them as sacrifices to the gods, nobles at San José Mogote demonstrated their ritual and political power as well as generosity to their communities. Human sacrifice was also a dramatic ritual performance in both its sacred and its violent qualities that would have bound people to the deities, ancestors, and other symbols of the community on whose behalf the sacrifice was realized." [371]

Limits on executive power imposed by religious agents[edit]

♠ Critique of executive by religious agents ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The relation between executive and religious agents are unclear although they appear to be intimately bound with one another. Sources do not suggest there is evidence for standardised religion during this period (standardised temples with private sacred rooms appear in later periods). A primary centre, San José Mogote, had two structures (19 and 28) which may have supported a temple, as well as a circular platform (structure 31). Smaller platform constructions were present at secondary centres, although the religious connection with the larger centres is not clear.[375]"Evidence from Mound 1 at San José Mogote indicates a major change in political discourse by the latter half of the Rosario phase, including the emergence of the first hereditary nobles in the Valley of Oaxaca. An important component of an emerging noble identity was the belief in their special ritual abilities that made them mediators between people and the sacred. The construction of high-status residences on Mound 1 transformed the structure from an area strictly for public ceremonial activities to an area combining public politico-religious buildings and elite residences in a distinct precinct. For the first time in Oaxaca a high-status residence was spatially and symbolically segregated from the rest of the community and incorporated into the ceremonial center. On a daily basis, people would have been constantly reminded of the sacred power of the ruling family as they viewed the inhabitants of Mound 1 from residential sectors of the site below. The linkage of noble status and divine authority would have been inscribed architecturally in Mound 1 by the close spatial association of the temples and the high-status residence." [376]

Mechanisms of Power Transfer (from one head of state to the next)[edit]

♠ Customary mechanism ♣ inferred present ♥ While there is evidence of an emerging elite the nature of leadership is still unclear. From Ritual: '"Despite the emergence of hereditary nobles, it is likely that the construction of monumental buildings, the enactment of public ceremonies like human sacrifice, and the pursuit of warfare were probably still seen in communal terms. Given that hereditary inequality emerged out of a more communal and less hierarchical discourse, it is reasonable to assume that the power of nobles was dependent on their being seen as acting on the behalf of their communities. These early nobles undoubtedly had ritual and political obligations to their communities like Zapotec and Mixtec rulers at the time of the Spanish Conquest. It is likely that nobles earned the allegiance of commoners through the sponsoring of public ceremonies, including ritual feasting, as well as through success in warfare and perhaps the gifting of certain luxury goods. Nobles deployed human sacrifice as another way to maintain allegiance since it was the most potent way in which the sacred covenant could be activated to petition deities for fertility and prosperity on behalf of the community. Given the physical setting of Monument 3, the audience for these sacrifices was probably restricted, much like the setting of Middle Formative iconography dealing with human sacrifice in other parts of Mesoamerica (A. Joyce 2008b). By taking captives and offering them as sacrifices to the gods, nobles at San José Mogote demonstrated their ritual and political power as well as generosity to their communities. Human sacrifice was also a dramatic ritual performance in both its sacred and its violent qualities that would have bound people to the deities, ancestors, and other symbols of the community on whose behalf the sacrifice was realized." [389] The chiefdoms during this period are also more complex than in the preceding San José and Guadalupe phases.[390][391]

Human sacrifice was also a dramatic ritual performance in both its sacred and its violent qualities that would have bound people to the deities, ancestors, and other symbols of the community on whose behalf the sacrifice was realized." [392] The chiefdoms during this period are also more complex than in the preceding San José and Guadalupe phases.[393][394]
♠ Number of power transitions ♣ suspected unknown ♥ While there is evidence of an emerging elite the nature of leadership is still unclear. From Ritual: '"Despite the emergence of hereditary nobles, it is likely that the construction of monumental buildings, the enactment of public ceremonies like human sacrifice, and the pursuit of warfare were probably still seen in communal terms. Given that hereditary inequality emerged out of a more communal and less hierarchical discourse, it is reasonable to assume that the power of nobles was dependent on their being seen as acting on the behalf of their communities. These early nobles undoubtedly had ritual and political obligations to their communities like Zapotec and Mixtec rulers at the time of the Spanish Conquest. It is likely that nobles earned the allegiance of commoners through the sponsoring of public ceremonies, including ritual feasting, as well as through success in warfare and perhaps the gifting of certain luxury goods. Nobles deployed human sacrifice as another way to maintain allegiance since it was the most potent way in which the sacred covenant could be activated to petition deities for fertility and prosperity on behalf of the community. Given the physical setting of Monument 3, the audience for these sacrifices was probably restricted, much like the setting of Middle Formative iconography dealing with human sacrifice in other parts of Mesoamerica (A. Joyce 2008b). By taking captives and offering them as sacrifices to the gods, nobles at San José Mogote demonstrated their ritual and political power as well as generosity to their communities. Human sacrifice was also a dramatic ritual performance in both its sacred and its violent qualities that would have bound people to the deities, ancestors, and other symbols of the community on whose behalf the sacrifice was realized." [395] The chiefdoms during this period are also more complex than in the preceding San José and Guadalupe phases.[396][397]

Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ inferred absent ♥ Although there was clearly community organization, probably supported/directed by elites the presence of a ‘state’ and the relationship between settlements is unclear at this time. Sources do not suggest there is evidence for full-time bureaucrats during this period.[404] AW discusses the administration structure - Possibly two could be inferred, based on the number of levels in the settlement hierarchy and the logistics required to import large limestone blocks for public buildings at San José Mogote (particularly Structure 19 which was possibly the largest and measured roughly 22x28m in extent). The stone came from a quarry at Rancho Matadamas which was 5km from San José Mogote.[405][406] This division may, however, be incorrect as it should not be assumed that the settlement hierarchy corresponds with the administrative hierarchy for this period (the sites may have been competing with each other rather than in dominant or subordinate administrative positions).[407] 1. Main administrative centre, corresponding with primary centres of the settlement hierarchy (e.g. San José Mogote)

2. Secondary administrative centres, corresponding with other smaller settlements with evidence for public architecture

Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The chiefdoms during this period are also more complex than in the preceding San José and Guadalupe phases.[454][455] The largest of the three chiefdoms in the valley at this time was in the Etla arm and consisted of 18-20 villages focused around the central settlement (San José Mogote).[456] It is assumed that this network of settlements formed a complex chiefdom because of their proximity and hierarchy in relation to San José Mogote (the largest settlement). It is unlikely that there were alliances between the three chiefdoms based on evidence for violence and inter-village raiding between the three arms of the valley.[457]Other smaller chiefly centers were Yegüih (in the eastern arm) and San Martin Tilcajete (in the southern arm).[458][459] [460]

♠ Local-level administrators ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Although there was clearly community organization, probably supported/directed by elites the presence of a ‘state’ and the relationship between settlements is unclear at this time. Sources do not suggest there is evidence for full-time bureaucrats during this period.[461] AW discusses the administration structure - Possibly two could be inferred, based on the number of levels in the settlement hierarchy and the logistics required to import large limestone blocks for public buildings at San José Mogote (particularly Structure 19 which was possibly the largest and measured roughly 22x28m in extent). The stone came from a quarry at Rancho Matadamas which was 5km from San José Mogote.[462][463] This division may, however, be incorrect as it should not be assumed that the settlement hierarchy corresponds with the administrative hierarchy for this period (the sites may have been competing with each other rather than in dominant or subordinate administrative positions).[464] 1. Legal System[edit]

♠ Multiple legal systems ♣ inferred absent ♥ From Phase I: A formal legal system, as associated buildings and lawyers, is assumed absent as the only evidence for writing during this period is from the carved stone slab (Monument 3) at San José Mogote[517] and the largest buildings were used as temples and elite residences.[518]

Inheritance System

♠ partible inheritance ♣ uncoded ♥ As there are no relevant written texts or supporting archaeological evidence there is no indication of the type of inheritance system employed at this time. Some archaeological evidence can be used to infer that high status was inherited by both male and female children but this cannot be used to infer equal inheritance rights. From Ritual: Cranial deformation - high status infants with cranial deformation found, children were either strapped to a fixed occipital board with a board on the forehead (tabular) or had a band tied tight around their head (anular). Tabluar deformation was the most common in the graves and could occur to male and female. One girl had annular deformation, possibly from another tribe, she was also buried atypically. [557]
♠ patrilineal inheritance ♣ uncoded ♥ As there are no relevant written texts or supporting archaeological evidence there is no indication of the type of inheritance system employed at this time. Equity[edit]

Sex Class and Race[edit]

Marriage

♠ Monogamy ♣ inferred present ♥ From Ritual: Burials - present - At Tomaltepec more than 60 graves found, some have two skeletons - the first evidence of husband and wife burials and people being treated as a pair rather than individuals. Evidence to suggest that people were dug up and reburied with their spouses. No infants or children - only those old enough to have gone through initiation rituals. Six men were buried in a kneeling position (all others extended prone) which was so tight they must have been tied or wrapped. They contained half the vessels in the cemetery and most of the jade beads and it is likely they were the community leaders. [562] In small cemetery in San Jose Magote remains of all ages found. Most were buried fully extended and face down. Nearly all adults had a single jade bead in the mouth. Husband and wife pairs were present. [563]
♠ Formal Elite Polygamy ♣ uncoded ♥
♠ Informal Elite Polygamy ♣ uncoded ♥
♠ Elite Concubinage ♣ uncoded ♥
♠ Typical number of extramarital partners ♣ uncoded ♥

Legal rights are different on the basis of:

♠ Gender ♣ uncoded ♥ From Phase I: A formal legal system, as associated buildings and lawyers, is assumed absent as the only evidence for writing during this period is from the carved stone slab (Monument 3) at San José Mogote[564] and the largest buildings were used as temples and elite residences.[565]
♠ Social class ♣ uncoded ♥ From Phase I: A formal legal system, as associated buildings and lawyers, is assumed absent as the only evidence for writing during this period is from the carved stone slab (Monument 3) at San José Mogote[566] and the largest buildings were used as temples and elite residences.[567] From Ritual: '"Despite the emergence of hereditary nobles, it is likely that the construction of monumental buildings, the enactment of public ceremonies like human sacrifice, and the pursuit of warfare were probably still seen in communal terms. Given that hereditary inequality emerged out of a more communal and less hierarchical discourse, it is reasonable to assume that the power of nobles was dependent on their being seen as acting on the behalf of their communities. These early nobles undoubtedly had ritual and political obligations to their communities like Zapotec and Mixtec rulers at the time of the Spanish Conquest. It is likely that nobles earned the allegiance of commoners through the sponsoring of public ceremonies, including ritual feasting, as well as through success in warfare and perhaps the gifting of certain luxury goods. Nobles deployed human sacrifice as another way to maintain allegiance since it was the most potent way in which the sacred covenant could be activated to petition deities for fertility and prosperity on behalf of the community. Given the physical setting of Monument 3, the audience for these sacrifices was probably restricted, much like the setting of Middle Formative iconography dealing with human sacrifice in other parts of Mesoamerica (A. Joyce 2008b). By taking captives and offering them as sacrifices to the gods, nobles at San José Mogote demonstrated their ritual and political power as well as generosity to their communities. Human sacrifice was also a dramatic ritual performance in both its sacred and its violent qualities that would have bound people to the deities, ancestors, and other symbols of the community on whose behalf the sacrifice was realized." [568]
♠ Ethnicity or race ♣ uncoded ♥ From Phase I: A formal legal system, as associated buildings and lawyers, is assumed absent as the only evidence for writing during this period is from the carved stone slab (Monument 3) at San José Mogote[569] and the largest buildings were used as temples and elite residences.[570]

Social Mobility[edit]

Status[edit]

Elite status

♠ elite status is hereditary ♣ inferred present ♥ ‘The brazier and tomb still do not constitute definitive evidence of a system of ranked descent groups, but they do suggest that by the Rosario phase there may have been more use of a rhetoric that claimed a connection between wealth, status, power, and ancestors. The use of the term "chiefdom" might be appropriate for this social formation, with the proviso that we still cannot be sure that ranked descent groups were part of the social matrix.’ [582] From Ritual: '"Despite the emergence of hereditary nobles, it is likely that the construction of monumental buildings, the enactment of public ceremonies like human sacrifice, and the pursuit of warfare were probably still seen in communal terms. Given that hereditary inequality emerged out of a more communal and less hierarchical discourse, it is reasonable to assume that the power of nobles was dependent on their being seen as acting on the behalf of their communities. These early nobles undoubtedly had ritual and political obligations to their communities like Zapotec and Mixtec rulers at the time of the Spanish Conquest. It is likely that nobles earned the allegiance of commoners through the sponsoring of public ceremonies, including ritual feasting, as well as through success in warfare and perhaps the gifting of certain luxury goods. Nobles deployed human sacrifice as another way to maintain allegiance since it was the most potent way in which the sacred covenant could be activated to petition deities for fertility and prosperity on behalf of the community. Given the physical setting of Monument 3, the audience for these sacrifices was probably restricted, much like the setting of Middle Formative iconography dealing with human sacrifice in other parts of Mesoamerica (A. Joyce 2008b). By taking captives and offering them as sacrifices to the gods, nobles at San José Mogote demonstrated their ritual and political power as well as generosity to their communities. Human sacrifice was also a dramatic ritual performance in both its sacred and its violent qualities that would have bound people to the deities, ancestors, and other symbols of the community on whose behalf the sacrifice was realized." [583] From Ritul: Cranial deformation - high status infants with cranial deformation found, children were either strapped to a fixed occipital board with a board on the forehead (tabular) or had a band tied tight around their head (anular). Tabluar deformation was the most common in the graves and could occur to male and female. One girl had annular deformation, possibly from another tribe, she was also buried atypically. [584]

Nobles deployed human sacrifice as another way to maintain allegiance since it was the most potent way in which the sacred covenant could be activated to petition deities for fertility and prosperity on behalf of the community. Given the physical setting of Monument 3, the audience for these sacrifices was probably restricted, much like the setting of Middle Formative iconography dealing with human sacrifice in other parts of Mesoamerica (A. Joyce 2008b). By taking captives and offering them as sacrifices to the gods, nobles at San José Mogote demonstrated their ritual and political power as well as generosity to their communities. Human sacrifice was also a dramatic ritual performance in both its sacred and its violent qualities that would have bound people to the deities, ancestors, and other symbols of the community on whose behalf the sacrifice was realized." [592]
♠ extreme status diminution ♣ uncoded ♥ While elite status has become hereditary it is unclear if it could still be attained. From Ritual: '"Despite the emergence of hereditary nobles, it is likely that the construction of monumental buildings, the enactment of public ceremonies like human sacrifice, and the pursuit of warfare were probably still seen in communal terms. Given that hereditary inequality emerged out of a more communal and less hierarchical discourse, it is reasonable to assume that the power of nobles was dependent on their being seen as acting on the behalf of their communities. These early nobles undoubtedly had ritual and political obligations to their communities like Zapotec and Mixtec rulers at the time of the Spanish Conquest. It is likely that nobles earned the allegiance of commoners through the sponsoring of public ceremonies, including ritual feasting, as well as through success in warfare and perhaps the gifting of certain luxury goods. Human sacrifice was also a dramatic ritual performance in both its sacred and its violent qualities that would have bound people to the deities, ancestors, and other symbols of the community on whose behalf the sacrifice was realized." [593]

Human sacrifice

♠ Prisoners ♣ present ♥ From Ritual: "By taking captives and offering them as sacrifices to the gods, nobles at San José Mogote demonstrated their ritual and political power as well as generosity to their communities." [595] A carved stone slab (Monument 3) at San Jose Mogote depicts a naked, mutilated captive, showing early evidence for prisoner capture and human sacrifice.[596] “Although people used autosacrificial bloodletting to make offerings to the deities as early as the Early Formative, the innovation of human sacrifice represents a new and more spectacular ritual practice used to communicate with the divine. It is likely that the performance of human sacrifice was restricted to Rosario-phase nobles, given that evidence for this practice was associated only with public buildings and elite residences and that only nobles carried out the ritual in later periods (Boone 1984; Schele & Miller 1986). Nobles acted as intermediaries between people and the sacred realm with human sacrifice as a new ritual form probably linked to warfare. Elaborate obsidian bloodletters and the anthropomorphic brazier suggest the presence of luxury goods and ritual paraphernalia restricted to nobles." [597] "Nobles acted as intermediaries between people and the sacred realm with human sacrifice as a new ritual form probably linked to warfare. Deification of Rulers[edit]

(‘gods’ is a shorthand for ‘supernatural agents’)

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ inferred present ♥ "Evidence from Mound 1 at San José Mogote indicates a major change in political discourse by the latter half of the Rosario phase, including the emergence of the first hereditary nobles in the Valley of Oaxaca. An important component of an emerging noble identity was the belief in their special ritual abilities that made them mediators between people and the sacred. The construction of high-status residences on Mound 1 transformed the structure from an area strictly for public ceremonial activities to an area combining public politico-religious buildings and elite residences in a distinct precinct. For the first time in Oaxaca a high-status residence was spatially and symbolically segregated from the rest of the community and incorporated into the ceremonial center. On a daily basis, people would have been constantly reminded of the sacred power of the ruling family as they viewed the inhabitants of Mound 1 from residential sectors of the site below. The linkage of noble status and divine authority would have been inscribed architecturally in Mound 1 by the close spatial association of the temples and the high-status residence." [615]

♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ inferred present ♥ "Evidence from Mound 1 at San José Mogote indicates a major change in political discourse by the latter half of the Rosario phase, including the emergence of the first hereditary nobles in the Valley of Oaxaca. An important component of an emerging noble identity was the belief in their special ritual abilities that made them mediators between people and the sacred. The construction of high-status residences on Mound 1 transformed the structure from an area strictly for public ceremonial activities to an area combining public politico-religious buildings and elite residences in a distinct precinct. For the first time in Oaxaca a high-status residence was spatially and symbolically segregated from the rest of the community and incorporated into the ceremonial center. On a daily basis, people would have been constantly reminded of the sacred power of the ruling family as they viewed the inhabitants of Mound 1 from residential sectors of the site below. The linkage of noble status and divine authority would have been inscribed architecturally in Mound 1 by the close spatial association of the temples and the high-status residence." [616]

♠ Rulers are descendants of gods ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Rulers become gods after death ♣ present ♥ "Royal ancestor worship was highly developed among the Zapotec, and because so much of their prehistory is known, we can see its components forming far back in time (Flannery and Marcus 1976, 1983b; Marcus 1978, 1989a). As long ago as 1150-850 B.C. one can see basic elements such as 'earth' and 'sky' depicted in stylized form on Oaxacan funerary ceramics (Marcus 1989a). 'Sky' was often represented by Cociyo, 'Lightning,' the most powerful and revered force in the Zapotec cosmos." [617]

♠ Automatic deification ♣ inferred present ♥ "Royal ancestor worship was highly developed among the Zapotec, and because so much of their prehistory is known, we can see its components forming far back in time (Flannery and Marcus 1976, 1983b; Marcus 1978, 1989a). As long ago as 1150-850 B.C. one can see basic elements such as 'earth' and 'sky' depicted in stylized form on Oaxacan funerary ceramics (Marcus 1989a). 'Sky' was often represented by Cociyo, 'Lightning,' the most powerful and revered force in the Zapotec cosmos." [618]

♠ Rulers are gods ♣ suspected unknown ♥

Human Enforcement of Morality

♠ care/harm ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ fairness/cheating ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ in-group loyalty ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ respect for authority ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Evidence from Mound 1 at San José Mogote indicates a major change in political discourse by the latter half of the Rosario phase, including the emergence of the first hereditary nobles in the Valley of Oaxaca. An important component of an emerging noble identity was the belief in their special ritual abilities that made them mediators between people and the sacred. The construction of high-status residences on Mound 1 transformed the structure from an area strictly for public ceremonial activities to an area combining public politico-religious buildings and elite residences in a distinct precinct. For the first time in Oaxaca a high-status residence was spatially and symbolically segregated from the rest of the community and incorporated into the ceremonial center. On a daily basis, people would have been constantly reminded of the sacred power of the ruling family as they viewed the inhabitants of Mound 1 from residential sectors of the site below. The linkage of noble status and divine authority would have been inscribed architecturally in Mound 1 by the close spatial association of the temples and the high-status residence." [634] However, no evidence could be found that this value was actually enforced. And even if respect for authority was enforced--which does seem likely--it's not obvious that this would have constituted enforcement of morality, as opposed to, for example, law enforcement, or, indeed, simple enforcement of the hierarchical order, with no connection to morality proper.
♠ purity ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ kinship ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ bravery ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Inferred from the likely centrality of warfare in Oaxacan ideology: "People communicated with the divine through ritual practices seen in Oaxaca for the first time, including human sacrifice and ballgame ceremonies. Nobility controlled both of these rituals, which were linked to another important trend: an increase in the significance of warfare." [635] However, no evidence could be found that this value was actually enforced.
