♠ Drinking water ♣ uncoded ♥ insufficient evidence

♠ provision of drinking water ♣ uncoded ♥ insufficient evidence

♠ Irrigation works ♣ inferred absent ♥ There is no evidence for the construction of canals during this period (although some small scale irrigation practices may have been used).[650] Evidence for terracing systems and irrigation appear in later periods after the founding of Monte Alban and the Zapotec polity. ‘As early as the San José phase, maize farmers in the Valley of Oaxaca supplemented water from rainfall with simple irrigation techniques (Flannery et al. 1967). Their repertoire included pot or well irrigation - a labor-intensive strategy that can be employed in many alluvial zones of the valley. One aspect of well irrigation is that it can be implemented by a single farmer or the members of one household. Early Oaxaca farmers also likely secured water by building modest check dams and small channels to divert water from seasonal runoffs and the temporary streams that run during the rainy season. These irrigation techniques also required no central management and little or no labor from outside the household. Neither large-scale irrigation systems nor massive networks of canals have been important during the long history of this region.’ [651]

♠ provision of irrigation works ♣ uncoded ♥

Entertainment and Knowledge Well-Being[edit]

♠ Funding for festivals ♣ inferred present ♥ From Ritual: "It is likely that nobles earned the allegiance of commoners through the sponsoring of public ceremonies, including ritual feasting, as well as through success in warfare and perhaps the gifting of certain luxury goods." [652]

♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ inferred present ♥ Structure 31 was a circular platform, 50-60 cm high, which may have been used for dancing or for combat.[653]

♠ Theatres ♣uncoded ♥ Structure 31 was a circular platform, 50-60 cm high, which may have been used for dancing or for combat.[654]
♠ Arenas ♣ uncoded ♥ Structure 31 was a circular platform, 50-60 cm high, which may have been used for dancing or for combat.[655]
♠ Ritual buildings/spaces ♣ present ♥ Numerous non-residential buildings were constructed on Mound 1 at San José Mogote during this period, including temple platforms (e.g. Structure 19) next to Monument 3 (the stone carving of a captive).[656] There is evidence for temples at primary and secondary settlements in the Etla arm of the valley. Priests may not have been full-time specialists, but the temples were considered important by people at the time (attested to by the focus of destruction in raids).[657]

Economy variables (polity-level)[edit]

State Income[edit]

♠ Tribute ♣ inferred absent ♥ There is no evidence for the presence of a taxation system Frome Phase I: The three chiefdoms were separate entities with no evidence for supra-polity relations.[662] The largest of the three chiefdoms in the valley at this time was in the Etla arm and consisted of 18-20 villages focused around the central settlement (San José Mogote).[663] It is assumed that this network of settlements formed a complex chiefdom because of their proximity and hierarchy in relation to San José Mogote (the largest settlement). It is unlikely that there were alliances between the three chiefdoms based on evidence for violence and inter-village raiding between the three arms of the valley.[664]Other smaller chiefly centers were Yegüih (in the eastern arm) and San Martin Tilcajete (in the southern arm).[665][666] [667]

♠ Staple ♣ uncoded ♥ There is no evidence for the presence of a taxation system
♠ Labor ♣ uncoded ♥ There is no evidence for the presence of a taxation system. While there is evidence of an emerging elite the nature of leadership is still unclear. Given the focus on raiding it is unlikely that rulers were separate from the ‘military’. From Phase I: More influential members of society also began to emerge more prominently during this period, who organised labour for the construction of large public structures and elaborate tombs. However, the types of buildings constructed suggest that elites could not yet use labour and resources just for their own personal gain (as elaborate as the elite residences were, Flannery and Marcus[668] believe that they could have been constructed by the elite family without the need for corvée labour). This next step in social hierarchy appeared later when the Zapotec state had been firmly established.[669] At least one level of military organisation is inferred, based on evidence for inter-village raiding.[670]
♠ Currency ♣ uncoded ♥ There is no evidence for the presence of a taxation system. From Phase I: Monetary items have not been found dating to this period.[671]

♠ Polity has obligation to other polity or group ♣ uncoded ♥ Frome Phase I: The three chiefdoms were separate entities with no evidence for supra-polity relations.[672] The largest of the three chiefdoms in the valley at this time was in the Etla arm and consisted of 18-20 villages focused around the central settlement (San José Mogote).[673] It is assumed that this network of settlements formed a complex chiefdom because of their proximity and hierarchy in relation to San José Mogote (the largest settlement). It is unlikely that there were alliances between the three chiefdoms based on evidence for violence and inter-village raiding between the three arms of the valley.[674]Other smaller chiefly centers were Yegüih (in the eastern arm) and San Martin Tilcajete (in the southern arm).[675][676] [677]
♠ Polity receives obligatory payment from other polity or group ♣ uncoded ♥ Frome Phase I: The three chiefdoms were separate entities with no evidence for supra-polity relations.[678] The largest of the three chiefdoms in the valley at this time was in the Etla arm and consisted of 18-20 villages focused around the central settlement (San José Mogote).[679] It is assumed that this network of settlements formed a complex chiefdom because of their proximity and hierarchy in relation to San José Mogote (the largest settlement). Wages and Costs[edit]

♠ Wages ♣ uncoded ♥ the type of remuneration provided for work is unknown. From Phase I: Monetary items have not been found dating to this period.[691]

♠ staple cost of principal source of carbohydrates ♣ uncoded ♥

♠ Top fortune ♣ uncoded ♥ Frome Phase I: The only evidence for accumulated wealth are from higher status burials.[692]

♠ Other wealth distribution ♣ ♥ By taking captives and offering them as sacrifices to the gods, nobles at San José Mogote demonstrated their ritual and political power as well as generosity to their communities. Human sacrifice was also a dramatic ritual performance in both its sacred and its violent qualities that would have bound people to the deities, ancestors, and other symbols of the community on whose behalf the sacrifice was realized." [693]
